## 1.7 Calisthenics Skills

### Beginner Skills
**Query:** "List all beginner calisthenics skills and progressions with: skill name, prerequisites, progression steps (from easiest to hardest), muscles worked. Include: pull-up progressions, push-up progressions, dip progressions, L-sit progressions."

**Response:**
```
COMPLETED BY CLAUDE (DETAILED VERSION)

## CALISTHENICS OVERVIEW & PHILOSOPHY

Calisthenics (from Greek kallos "beauty" + sthenos "strength") is the art of using body weight as resistance for building strength, muscle, and extraordinary body control. Unlike weight training where you add external load, calisthenics progression happens through **leverage manipulation**—changing body position to make exercises harder while using the same resistance (your body).

**Why Calisthenics?**
- **No equipment required**: Can train anywhere—parks, home, hotel rooms
- **Joint-friendly progression**: Gradually increases difficulty without sudden load jumps
- **Builds relative strength**: Strength-to-bodyweight ratio prioritized
- **Develops body control**: Proprioception and coordination improve dramatically
- **Transferable skills**: Directly applicable to sports, martial arts, daily life
- **Impressive skills**: Muscle-ups, handstands, levers are visually striking feats

**Fundamental Principle: Progressions**
Every calisthenics exercise follows a progression—a series of increasingly difficult variations. You progress when you can perform the current level with **good form for the required reps/time**. This ensures continuous challenge without the need for weights.

**The Big 5 Movement Patterns:**
1. **Horizontal Push** (Push-ups) → Chest, triceps, front delts
2. **Horizontal Pull** (Rows) → Back, biceps, rear delts
3. **Vertical Push** (Dips/Handstand Push-ups) → Shoulders, triceps, chest
4. **Vertical Pull** (Pull-ups) → Lats, biceps, grip
5. **Legs/Core** (Squats, L-sits) → Complete lower body and core

**Key Training Principles:**
- **Form over reps**: Never sacrifice technique to complete more reps—it builds bad habits and increases injury risk
- **Full range of motion (ROM)**: Partial reps = partial gains. Go through complete ROM on every rep.
- **Controlled tempo**: Use 1-2 second concentric, 2-3 second eccentric. No bouncing or swinging.
- **Progressive overload**: Once you hit the target reps/time, move to the next progression
- **Rest and recovery**: Calisthenics is demanding—allow 48-72 hours between training same movements
- **Patience**: Progressions may take weeks or months. This is normal. Trust the process.

---

## PULL-UP PROGRESSIONS

**The pull-up is arguably the most important upper body pulling exercise**, building the lats, biceps, forearms, and grip. A strong pull-up is the foundation for advanced skills like muscle-ups, front levers, and one-arm pull-ups.

**Muscles Worked:** Latissimus dorsi (primary), biceps brachii, brachialis, brachioradialis, teres major, rhomboids, rear deltoids, core (stabilization), forearms (grip)

**Why Many People Can't Do Pull-Ups:**
- Weakness in lats and/or biceps
- Excess body weight (every extra pound is extra resistance)
- Never developed the neuromuscular pattern
- Lack of scapular control (can't initiate the movement properly)

**Chin-Up vs. Pull-Up:**
- **Chin-up** (palms facing you): More bicep emphasis, usually easier, excellent for beginners
- **Pull-up** (palms facing away): More lat emphasis, typically harder, standard calisthenics version
- Both build back and arms—use both in training

| Step | Exercise | Target to Progress | Form Cues & Details |
|------|----------|-------------------|---------------------|
| 1 | **Dead Hang** | 30-60 seconds | Hang from bar with straight arms, shoulders packed down (not shrugging to ears). Grip just outside shoulder width. **Builds grip strength and prepares tendons.** If can't hold 30 sec, grip is limiting factor—address with grip work. |
| 2 | **Active Hang (Scapular Pulls)** | 10-15 controlled reps | From dead hang, pull shoulder blades down and together (depress and retract), raising body 1-2 inches without bending elbows. **Teaches scapular initiation**—pull-ups start from the shoulder blades, not the arms. Critical step many skip. |
| 3 | **Inverted Rows (Australian Pull-Ups)** | 15-20 reps (body at 45°) | Set bar at waist height, grip overhand, body straight at angle, pull chest to bar. Lower surface = harder. **Builds horizontal pulling strength and rowing pattern.** Can use TRX, rings, or bar on rack. Excellent builder. |
| 4 | **Band-Assisted Pull-Ups (Heavy Band)** | 8-10 reps | Loop thick resistance band over bar, place foot or knee in band, perform full pull-ups. Band provides most assistance at bottom where you're weakest. **Allows practice of full ROM pull-up movement.** Start with heavy band. |
| 5 | **Band-Assisted Pull-Ups (Medium/Light Band)** | 8-10 reps | Progress to thinner bands providing less assistance. Continue until using thinnest band available. Each band reduction is significant progress. |
| 6 | **Negative (Eccentric) Pull-Ups** | 5-8 reps × 5 sec lowering | Jump or step to top position (chin over bar), **lower slowly for 5 seconds** fighting gravity. Reset and repeat. **Eccentric strength transfers to concentric.** The most effective progression step. Build up to 8 reps of slow 5-sec negatives. |
| 7 | **Jumping Pull-Up + Slow Negative** | 5-8 reps | Jump to assist the concentric (up) phase, control the negative (down). Combines explosive pulling with eccentric work. |
| 8 | **Half Pull-Ups (Top Half)** | 8-10 reps | From dead hang, pull to halfway point (elbows at 90°), return to hang. Builds strength in the range just before the full movement. |
| 9 | **Full Pull-Up** | 1+ reps | Dead hang start, pull until chin clears bar, lower with control. **Achievement unlocked!** Now build volume—aim for 3 sets of 5-8 before moving to advanced variations. |

**Pull-Up Tips:**
- **Grip**: Start with shoulder-width or slightly wider. False grip (thumb over) builds grip strength but underhand grip is usually easier.
- **Initiate with lats**: Think "elbows to hips" rather than "pull chin to bar"—this cues the lats properly.
- **Avoid kipping**: Strict form builds real strength. Kipping is a different skill.
- **Common mistake**: Starting with arms (biceps) instead of scapula. Always initiate scapular depression first.

---

## PUSH-UP PROGRESSIONS

**The push-up is the foundational horizontal pushing exercise**, training chest, triceps, and shoulders while demanding core stability. Mastery of the push-up unlocks dozens of variations and is prerequisite for planche work.

**Muscles Worked:** Pectoralis major (primary), anterior deltoids, triceps brachii, serratus anterior, core (stabilization), rotator cuff (stabilization)

**Why the Push-Up Is Superior to Bench Press (for many goals):**
- Trains the serratus anterior (scapular movement) which bench press locks out
- Requires core stability (closed kinetic chain)
- Builds functional pushing patterns used in sports and life
- Infinite progressions—can always make harder without equipment

| Step | Exercise | Target to Progress | Form Cues & Details |
|------|----------|-------------------|---------------------|
| 1 | **Wall Push-Ups** | 15-20 reps | Stand arm's length from wall, hands shoulder-width at shoulder height, lower chest toward wall, push back. **Most basic progression.** Focus on form—straight body line, elbows at 45° angle (not flared 90°), full ROM. |
| 2 | **Incline Push-Ups (High Surface)** | 15-20 reps | Hands on sturdy elevated surface (table, counter, high bench), body straight from head to heels, perform push-up. **Lower the surface as you progress.** Counter → chair → bench → low step → floor. |
| 3 | **Incline Push-Ups (Lower Surface)** | 15-20 reps | Same as above with hands on lower surface (bench, sturdy chair). Closer to floor = harder. Maintain rigid body—don't sag hips or pike up. |
| 4 | **Knee Push-Ups** | 15-20 reps | On floor, knees down, hands shoulder-width, body straight from knees to head. **Keep core engaged**—don't collapse at hips. Full ROM: chest touches (or nearly touches) floor. Often called "modified push-ups." Better progression than staying stuck on incline. |
| 5 | **Negative Push-Ups (Eccentric)** | 8-10 reps × 5 sec lowering | Start in top of push-up position, **lower slowly for 5 seconds** to floor, reset to top (use knees if needed), repeat. Builds eccentric strength that transfers to the full movement. Critical step. |
| 6 | **Half Push-Ups** | 10-15 reps | Full push-up position, lower only halfway (until elbows at 90°), push back up. Builds strength in the sticking point range. |
| 7 | **Full Push-Up** | 10+ reps | Hands shoulder-width, body straight head to heels, lower until chest nearly touches floor, push back up. **Elbows at 45° angle** (not flared to 90° which stresses shoulders). Squeeze glutes, brace core. This is the foundation—master it before variations. |
| 8 | **Diamond Push-Up** | 10+ reps | Hands together forming diamond shape with thumbs and index fingers, positioned under chest. **Tricep emphasis** while still working chest. Significantly harder than regular push-ups. |

**Push-Up Form Checklist:**
- **Body line**: Straight from ears through shoulders, hips, knees, ankles. No sagging hips or piking up.
- **Elbow angle**: 45° from body (not flared to 90° which stresses shoulder joint)
- **Hand position**: Just outside shoulder width, fingers forward or slightly turned out
- **Scapulae**: Protract at top (push through), retract at bottom
- **Core**: Engaged throughout—think "hollow body" position
- **Full ROM**: Chest to 1-2 inches from floor (or touches), full lockout at top

**Additional Push-Up Variations (Post-Mastery):**
- *Wide push-ups* (more chest)
- *Close grip push-ups* (more tricep)
- *Archer push-ups* (unilateral progression toward one-arm)
- *Decline push-ups* (feet elevated—more shoulder/upper chest)
- *Pseudo planche push-ups* (hands by hips—planche prep)
- *Clapping push-ups* (explosive power)

---

## DIP PROGRESSIONS

**The dip is the premier vertical pushing exercise**, building tremendous triceps, chest, and shoulder strength. It's a prerequisite for muscle-ups and allows heavy loading through weighted variations.

**Muscles Worked:** Triceps brachii (primary), pectoralis major (lower emphasis), anterior deltoids, core (stabilization)

**Chest Dip vs. Tricep Dip:**
- **Tricep-focused**: Upright torso, elbows close to body, minimal forward lean
- **Chest-focused**: Forward lean, elbows slightly flared, deeper ROM
- Both are valuable—context determines which to use

| Step | Exercise | Target to Progress | Form Cues & Details |
|------|----------|-------------------|---------------------|
| 1 | **Support Hold (Parallel Bars or Chairs)** | 30 seconds | Jump or step to top of dips position (arms straight, body supported on bars), hold. **Builds stability and gets elbows/shoulders accustomed to position.** Shoulders down, core tight, don't shrug. |
| 2 | **Bench Dips (Feet on Floor)** | 12-15 reps | Hands on bench/chair behind you, feet on floor, lower by bending elbows until upper arms parallel to floor, push up. **Entry-level dip movement.** Keep back close to bench. Not a replacement for parallel bar dips but useful regression. |
| 3 | **Bench Dips (Feet Elevated)** | 12-15 reps | Same as above but feet on another bench/chair, increasing load. Keep core engaged, don't let hips sag away from bench. |
| 4 | **Band-Assisted Parallel Bar Dips** | 8-10 reps | Loop band over parallel bars, knees or feet in band, perform full dips with band assistance. **Allows practice of proper dip movement pattern.** Use thick band initially, progress to thinner bands. |
| 5 | **Negative Dips (Eccentric)** | 5-8 reps × 5 sec lowering | Jump or step to top position on parallel bars, **lower slowly for 5 seconds** until upper arms parallel to floor (or slightly below), jump back to top, repeat. **Most effective progression step.** Control the descent completely. |
| 6 | **Half Dips** | 8-10 reps | On parallel bars, lower only halfway (less than parallel), push back up. Builds strength in upper portion of movement. |
| 7 | **Full Parallel Bar Dips** | 5+ reps | Start locked out at top, lower until upper arms at least parallel to floor (deeper is more challenging), push up to lockout. **Major achievement.** Keep core tight, control the entire movement. Slight forward lean is natural. |

**Dip Technique Tips:**
- **Depth**: At minimum, upper arms parallel to floor. Deeper = more chest/shoulder, but also more shoulder stress. Find your comfortable depth.
- **Elbow flare**: Elbows at ~45° from body. Full flare stresses shoulders.
- **Forward lean**: Slight lean is natural. More lean = more chest. Upright = more triceps.
- **Shoulder safety**: If dips cause shoulder pain, reduce depth or skip them—they're not for everyone's anatomy.
- **Lockout**: Full arm extension at top. Controlled, not hyperextended.

---

## L-SIT PROGRESSIONS

**The L-sit is a fundamental isometric hold** that builds hip flexor strength, core endurance, and teaches total body tension. It's foundational for V-sit, manna, and press to handstand.

**Muscles Worked:** Hip flexors (iliopsoas, rectus femoris—primary), rectus abdominis, obliques, triceps (pressing down), quadriceps (keeping legs straight), serratus anterior (scapular depression)

**Why Most People Struggle With L-Sit:**
- Weak hip flexors (rarely trained directly)
- Tight hamstrings (can't keep legs straight while hip flexed)
- Weak triceps/shoulders (can't maintain support)
- Don't understand the technique (it's about pushing down AND compressing)

| Step | Exercise | Target to Progress | Form Cues & Details |
|------|----------|-------------------|---------------------|
| 1 | **Seated Leg Raises (Active Compression)** | 15 reps × 5 sec holds | Sit on floor, legs straight in front, hands beside hips, **lift legs as high as possible without leaning back**, hold 5 sec, lower. **Teaches hip flexor compression** needed for L-sit. Don't cheat by leaning back. |
| 2 | **Tucked L-Sit (Feet on Floor)** | 30 seconds | On parallettes or hands on floor, lift hips off ground, knees tucked to chest, feet still lightly touching floor. **Learn to support bodyweight while in tucked position.** Push shoulders down hard (scapular depression). |
| 3 | **One-Foot Supported L-Sit** | 15 seconds each side | Same as above but one leg extended forward (off ground), other foot lightly touching. **Transition step.** Alternate which leg is supported. |
| 4 | **Tucked L-Sit (Feet Off Floor)** | 15-20 seconds | Hands on parallettes or floor, lift hips, knees tucked tight to chest, **both feet completely off floor**. This is the first "real" L-sit variation. Keep tucked tight, push down hard through arms. |
| 5 | **One-Leg Extended L-Sit** | 10-15 seconds each side | Tucked L-sit but extend one leg straight out, keep other tucked. **Huge hip flexor demand on extended leg.** Alternate legs. Point toes. |
| 6 | **Full L-Sit** | 5+ seconds (build to 15-30 sec) | Both legs extended straight in front, hips off ground, forming "L" shape. **The goal.** Requires significant hip flexor strength, hamstring flexibility, and pressing strength. Point toes. Build hold time gradually. |

**L-Sit Tips:**
- **Equipment**: Parallettes or push-up handles raise hands off ground, making L-sit much easier (more clearance for legs). Use them initially.
- **Scapular depression**: Push shoulders DOWN hard throughout. Shrugged shoulders = failing.
- **Hip flexor cramping**: Very common. It means your hip flexors are weak. Keep training—they'll adapt.
- **Hamstring tightness**: If you can't straighten legs, work on seated hamstring stretches alongside L-sit practice.
- **Compression work**: L-sit requires compressing (lifting) straight legs. Practice seated leg raises daily.

---

## SQUAT PROGRESSIONS (BODYWEIGHT)

**The squat is the foundational lower body movement**, building the quadriceps, glutes, and hamstrings while requiring ankle, hip, and thoracic mobility.

**Muscles Worked:** Quadriceps (primary), glutes, hamstrings, adductors, core (stabilization), calves

| Step | Exercise | Target to Progress | Form Cues & Details |
|------|----------|-------------------|---------------------|
| 1 | **Assisted Squat (Hold Support)** | 15-20 reps | Hold onto doorframe, pole, or TRX, squat down using support for balance/assistance. **For those who can't yet perform bodyweight squat.** Focus on depth and form while building strength. |
| 2 | **Box Squat (Sit on Box)** | 15-20 reps | Squat down to sit on box/chair, stand back up. **Provides consistent depth target** and confidence at bottom. Lower the box as strength improves. |
| 3 | **Bodyweight Squat** | 20+ reps | Feet shoulder-width or slightly wider, toes slightly out, squat until hip crease below knee level, stand up. **Master this before advanced variations.** Chest up, knees tracking over toes, weight through full foot. |
| 4 | **Pause Squat (3 sec at bottom)** | 15 reps | Full bodyweight squat but pause for 3 seconds in the bottom position. **Eliminates stretch reflex, builds strength from bottom position.** Harder than it sounds. |
| 5 | **Jump Squat** | 15 reps | Squat down, explode up into a jump, land softly, immediately descend into next rep. **Builds explosive power.** Land with soft knees, absorb impact. |
| 6 | **Bulgarian Split Squat** | 10 each leg | Rear foot elevated on bench/chair, front foot forward. Squat on front leg until back knee nearly touches floor. **Unilateral leg strength**—huge for athleticism and addressing imbalances. Step toward pistol squat. |

**Squat Form Essentials:**
- **Depth**: Hip crease below knee (full depth). Don't half-squat.
- **Feet**: Shoulder width or slightly wider, toes pointed out 15-30°
- **Knees**: Track over toes throughout—don't cave inward (valgus)
- **Spine**: Neutral—slight natural arch in lower back, chest up
- **Weight distribution**: Through full foot—can wiggle toes at bottom
- **Common fault**: Knees caving in (weak glutes/abductors), heels rising (tight ankles)

---

## BEGINNER TRAINING RECOMMENDATIONS

**Frequency:** 3 sessions per week (e.g., Mon/Wed/Fri), allowing 48hr recovery

**Sample Beginner Full-Body Routine:**

| Exercise | Sets × Reps | Rest |
|----------|-------------|------|
| Bodyweight Squats (or progression) | 3 × 8-15 | 60 sec |
| Push-Up Progression | 3 × 5-12 | 60-90 sec |
| Row Progression (Inverted Rows) | 3 × 5-12 | 60-90 sec |
| Dip Progression | 3 × 5-10 | 60-90 sec |
| Pull-Up Progression | 3 × 3-8 | 90-120 sec |
| L-Sit Progression | 3 × 10-30 sec | 60 sec |

**Progression Rules:**
- Hit the upper end of rep/time range with good form → move to next progression
- Can't complete minimum reps → stay at current level or regress
- Progress one exercise at a time—don't rush multiple jumps simultaneously

**Timeline to Basic Proficiency:**
- First pull-up: 4-12 weeks (depends on starting point, bodyweight)
- 10 proper push-ups: 2-8 weeks
- First parallel bar dip: 4-12 weeks
- 5-second L-sit: 4-16 weeks
- The journey from zero to basic competency typically takes 2-6 months with consistency

**Sources:**
- Overcoming Gravity (Steven Low) - Comprehensive calisthenics programming
- Convict Conditioning (Paul Wade) - Progressive calisthenics method
- FitnessFAQs / Calisthenicmovement - Modern calisthenics resources
- NSCA position stands on bodyweight training
- ExRx.net - Bodyweight exercise database
```

### Intermediate Skills
**Query:** "List all intermediate calisthenics skills with: skill name, prerequisites, progression steps, muscles worked. Include: muscle-up, pistol squat, dragon flag, front lever progressions."

**Response:**
```
COMPLETED BY CLAUDE (DETAILED VERSION)

## INTERMEDIATE CALISTHENICS OVERVIEW

Intermediate calisthenics marks the transition from basic exercises to **impressive skill work**. This is where calisthenics becomes truly unique—the skills at this level cannot be replicated with weights and require specific neurological adaptations.

**What Makes Someone "Intermediate"?**
You've moved beyond beginner when you can:
- Perform 15+ strict pull-ups
- Perform 30+ push-ups with perfect form
- Perform 15+ parallel bar dips
- Hold a 15+ second L-sit
- Perform 20+ bodyweight squats with full depth

**The Intermediate Training Mindset:**
- **Skill acquisition** becomes as important as strength building
- **Movement quality** over quantity—sloppy reps build sloppy skills
- **Patience**: These skills take months to years, not weeks
- **Consistency**: Regular practice with proper progressions beats occasional intense sessions
- **Injury prevention**: Advanced skills stress joints—prehab and warmups are non-negotiable

---

## MUSCLE-UP PROGRESSIONS

**The muscle-up is the gateway skill** that transforms a pull-up and dip into a single fluid movement, transitioning from below the bar to above it. It's the moment when "training" becomes "skill" and opens the door to bar gymnastics.

**Muscles Worked:** Latissimus dorsi (pull phase), pectoralis major (transition + push), triceps brachii (push phase), biceps brachii (pull phase), anterior deltoids (transition + push), core (stabilization throughout)

**Prerequisites (Non-Negotiable):**
- 10-15 strict pull-ups (chin above bar, no kipping)
- 15-20 straight bar dips (critical—parallel bar dips don't fully transfer)
- Understanding of the "transition" movement

**The Three Phases of Muscle-Up:**
1. **Pull Phase**: High pull-up pulling bar to lower chest/sternum (NOT just chin to bar)
2. **Transition Phase**: Rotating over the bar—the hardest and most technical part
3. **Push Phase**: Dip to push body above bar to lockout

**Why Most People Fail:**
- Can't pull high enough (bar needs to reach lower chest, not chin)
- Don't lean forward during transition (stay too upright)
- Elbows go behind the bar instead of staying over it
- Treat it as two separate movements instead of one fluid motion

| Step | Exercise | Target to Progress | Detailed Technique |
|------|----------|-------------------|-------------------|
| 1 | **High Pull-Ups (Chest to Bar)** | 8-10 reps | Pull until bar touches lower sternum/upper abdomen, not just chin above bar. **You MUST be able to pull this high** or you won't have room to transition. Think "pull bar to chest" not "pull chin to bar." |
| 2 | **Explosive Pull-Ups** | 5-8 reps | Pull explosively from dead hang, generating maximum power to pull as high as possible. Can add slight kip to understand timing. **Builds the explosive pulling power** needed for muscle-up. |
| 3 | **Straight Bar Dips** | 10-15 reps | Dips ON a straight bar (not parallel bars). Hands on top of bar, lower until bar touches mid-chest, press up to lockout. **This is the top half of the muscle-up.** Different from parallel bar dips—learn this specifically. |
| 4 | **Transition Practice (Jumping MU)** | 5-8 reps | Use low bar you can jump to, jump into the muscle-up focusing on the transition. **Learn the rotation pattern** without strength being the limiter. Feel how elbows roll over the bar. |
| 5 | **Band-Assisted Muscle-Up** | 5-8 reps | Thick band over bar, foot in band. Perform full muscle-ups with band assistance. **Practice the full movement pattern** while building strength. Progress to thinner bands. |
| 6 | **Negative Muscle-Up** | 3-5 reps (5+ sec lower) | Start above bar (in dip position), slowly lower through transition and pull phase to dead hang. **Eccentric muscle-ups build strength through the sticking point.** Emphasize slow, controlled transition phase. |
| 7 | **Kipping Muscle-Up** | 1+ rep | Use hip drive and swing to assist pulling high and transitioning. **Not the end goal** but a valid skill and useful stepping stone. Many gymnasts use kipping. Learn proper swing timing. |
| 8 | **Strict Muscle-Up** | 1+ rep | Dead hang start, explosive pull to chest level, forward lean into transition, push out to lockout—**all without kip or swing**. The ultimate bar skill. Build to multiple reps. |

**Muscle-Up Technique Keys:**
- **False grip** (wrist over bar) makes transition easier—practice hanging in false grip
- **Lean forward** as you transition—your chest should come over the bar, not stay behind it
- **Elbows together**: During transition, keep elbows close and forward, not flared
- **It's ONE movement**: Pull high AND lean forward simultaneously, not sequentially
- **Practice daily**: 5-10 minutes of muscle-up work even on rest days accelerates learning

---

## PISTOL SQUAT PROGRESSIONS

**The pistol squat (single-leg squat)** is the ultimate test of unilateral lower body strength, hip mobility, ankle mobility, and balance. It's a full-depth squat on one leg with the other leg extended in front.

**Muscles Worked:** Quadriceps (primary—massive demand), glutes, hamstrings, hip flexors (holding non-working leg up), calves, core (balance)

**Prerequisites:**
- 20+ full depth bodyweight squats
- Adequate ankle dorsiflexion mobility
- Hip flexor strength to hold extended leg
- Basic balance/proprioception

**Why Pistols Are So Challenging:**
1. **Strength**: Your quads must move 100% of body weight (vs. ~50% each leg in bilateral squat)
2. **Ankle mobility**: Extreme dorsiflexion needed to stay balanced—often the limiting factor
3. **Hip flexor strength**: Must hold extended leg at parallel throughout—very demanding
4. **Balance**: Single-leg stance on a moving surface (your body)
5. **Coordination**: All the above must work simultaneously

| Step | Exercise | Target to Progress | Detailed Technique |
|------|----------|-------------------|-------------------|
| 1 | **Assisted Pistol (Hold Support)** | 5-8 each leg | Hold TRX, doorframe, or pole for balance/assistance. Perform full ROM single-leg squat. **Learn the movement pattern** with support. Use as little assistance as possible. |
| 2 | **Box Pistol (Sit to Box)** | 5-8 each leg | Lower to sit on box/chair, then stand up on single leg. Lower the box height as strength increases. **Provides consistent depth target** and removes fear of falling. |
| 3 | **Negative Pistol (Eccentric Only)** | 5 each leg (5 sec lower) | Lower slowly on one leg with full control for 5 seconds until sitting at bottom. Use hands to stand back up. **Builds eccentric strength** through the hardest range. |
| 4 | **Pistol to Low Box/Pad** | 3-5 each leg | Descend into full pistol but stop when bottom touches low target (pad, folded towel), immediately stand back up. **Reduces ROM slightly** while practicing the full movement. Lower the target as you progress. |
| 5 | **Counterweight Pistol** | 3-5 each leg | Hold weight (5-15 lbs) in front of body with arms extended. The counterweight shifts center of mass forward, making balance easier. **Excellent intermediate step.** Reduce weight as strength improves. |
| 6 | **Partial Pistol (Elevated Surface)** | 5 each leg | Stand on elevated surface (step, box) so non-working leg can hang below platform level. **Reduces hip flexor demand** of holding leg horizontal. |
| 7 | **Full Pistol Squat** | 1+ each leg | From standing on one leg, descend all the way until hamstring touches calf, non-working leg extended parallel to floor, stand back up without assistance. **Achievement unlocked!** Build reps and eventually add weight. |

**Pistol Mobility Requirements:**
- **Ankle dorsiflexion**: Can you touch knee 4+ inches past toes while keeping heel down? If not, work on ankle mobility.
- **Hip flexion**: Can you hold leg parallel while seated? If not, do seated leg raises and stretches.
- **Hip flexor flexibility**: Tight hip flexors limit depth. Stretch regularly.

**Common Pistol Mistakes:**
- **Heel rising**: Usually ankle mobility issue—elevate heels on plate as training tool
- **Falling backward**: Lack of ankle mobility or not leaning torso forward enough
- **Extended leg dropping**: Weak hip flexors—do compression work
- **Knee caving in**: Weak glute medius—add single-leg glute bridges, clamshells

---

## DRAGON FLAG PROGRESSIONS

**The dragon flag (made famous by Bruce Lee)** is an extreme core exercise that looks like a lying leg raise but requires total body tension to maintain a rigid line from shoulders to toes. It's primarily an anti-extension exercise that builds incredible core strength.

**Muscles Worked:** Rectus abdominis (primary—intense anti-extension demand), obliques, hip flexors (maintaining hip extension while elevated), erector spinae (isometric stabilization), lats (pressing down on bench)

**Prerequisites:**
- 20 lying leg raises with control
- 60 second hollow body hold
- Strong pressing ability (pushing down on bench)

**What Makes Dragon Flags Unique:**
Unlike crunches or leg raises, the dragon flag requires you to **lift and hold your entire body rigid** while only shoulders contact the bench. The lower back especially must resist extension forces—making this one of the most demanding anti-extension exercises.

| Step | Exercise | Target to Progress | Detailed Technique |
|------|----------|-------------------|-------------------|
| 1 | **Lying Leg Raises** | 20 reps | Lie on back, hands under hips or holding bench behind head, raise legs to vertical keeping lower back pressed flat. **Prerequisite core strength.** |
| 2 | **Hollow Body Hold** | 60 seconds | Lie on back, arms by ears, legs straight, lift shoulders and legs, press lower back into floor, hold "banana" shape. **Teaches the body line needed for dragon flag.** Zero space between lower back and floor. |
| 3 | **Tucked Dragon Flag Negative** | 5 reps × 5 sec | Lie on bench, grip behind head, raise body to vertical (tucked knees), **slowly lower for 5 seconds** keeping body rigid (not letting hips pike), touch back to bench at the end. **Eccentric strength builder.** Control the descent. |
| 4 | **Tucked Dragon Flag (Full Rep)** | 8-10 reps | Same as above but raise AND lower under control. Knees tucked to chest, body forms straight line from knees to shoulders at bottom of movement. **Keep hips extended**—don't pike. |
| 5 | **Advanced Tuck Dragon Flag** | 8-10 reps | Same movement but with knees less tucked—thighs at 90° or greater from torso. **Increases lever length and difficulty.** Maintain body line. |
| 6 | **One-Leg Dragon Flag** | 5-8 each side | One leg extended fully, one leg tucked. Alternate which leg is extended. **Asymmetric loading** challenges core differently. |
| 7 | **Straddle Dragon Flag** | 5-8 reps | Both legs extended but spread wide (straddled). **Reduces lever length** compared to full but more than tuck. |
| 8 | **Full Dragon Flag** | 3-5 reps | Body completely straight from shoulders to toes, raise to vertical, lower slowly maintaining rigid line until just above bench, raise again. **The peak of anti-extension core work.** Entire body perfectly straight throughout. |

**Dragon Flag Form Keys:**
- **Grip bench hard**: Press down with arms/lats to create a stable base
- **Shoulders stay pinned**: Only contact point is upper back/shoulders—never lower back
- **Hips extended**: Body forms straight line—if hips pike, you're compensating
- **Control the negative**: The lowering phase is where the magic happens
- **Don't go to failure**: Stop when form breaks, not when muscles quit

---

## FRONT LEVER PROGRESSIONS

**The front lever is one of the most impressive static holds**—suspending the body horizontally face-up from a bar. It requires tremendous lat strength in a straight-arm position plus full-body tension.

**Muscles Worked:** Latissimus dorsi (primary—straight-arm pulling), posterior deltoids, biceps (isometric), rhomboids, teres major, core (entire), glutes (body alignment)

**Prerequisites:**
- 15+ pull-ups
- Strong scapular control (active hangs)
- 30 second L-sit
- Dragon flag capability (similar core demand)

**Why Front Lever Is So Difficult:**
The front lever requires **straight-arm lat strength**—pulling with elbows locked. This is much harder than bent-arm pulling (pull-ups) because you can't use biceps effectively. The lever (your body length) creates tremendous torque on the shoulders.

**Straight-Arm Strength Concept:**
In bent-arm pulling (pull-ups), your biceps assist. In straight-arm pulling (front lever), it's almost entirely lats. This is why someone who can do 20 pull-ups might not hold a tucked front lever for 5 seconds.

| Step | Exercise | Target to Progress | Detailed Technique |
|------|----------|-------------------|-------------------|
| 1 | **Active Hang / Scapular Pulls** | 15 reps | From dead hang, depress and retract scapulae, raising body 1-2" without bending elbows. **Builds straight-arm pulling pattern** needed for front lever. Foundation step. |
| 2 | **Tucked Front Lever** | 15-20 seconds | Hang from bar, pull body to horizontal (face up) with knees tucked tightly to chest. **Shortest lever** makes this the easiest variation. Keep scapulae retracted and depressed. |
| 3 | **Advanced Tuck Front Lever** | 10-15 seconds | Same as above but knees less tucked—thighs at 90° from torso instead of fully tucked. **Increases lever length.** Must maintain horizontal body. |
| 4 | **Single-Leg Front Lever** | 10 seconds each | One leg extended, one leg tucked. Alternate. **Asymmetric loading** toward full lever. |
| 5 | **Straddle Front Lever** | 5-10 seconds | Legs extended but spread wide (straddled). **Reduced lever length** compared to full but significant progress. |
| 6 | **Half-Lay Front Lever** | 5-10 seconds | Legs together but knees bent 90° (body forms "7" shape when viewed from side). Transition step to full. |
| 7 | **Full Front Lever** | 3-5 seconds | Body completely horizontal, face up, arms straight, legs together and extended. **The ultimate straight-arm pulling skill.** Takes months to years to achieve. |

**Front Lever Training Tips:**
- **Supplementary exercises**: Straight-arm lat pulldowns, dragon flags, toes-to-bar (straight legs)
- **Frequency**: Can practice front lever progressions 3-5x per week—it's skill work
- **Negatives are valuable**: Lower from inverted hang to horizontal to work eccentric strength
- **Rows help**: Front lever rows (pulling to bar while in tucked front lever position) build specific strength
- **Band assist**: Loop band over bar and under hips to reduce load while learning body position

**Common Front Lever Mistakes:**
- **Hips sagging**: Core not engaged—body should be perfectly straight
- **Bent arms**: Defeats the purpose—keep elbows locked
- **Shrugged shoulders**: Shoulders should be depressed (pulled down)
- **Progressing too fast**: Build time in each progression before moving on

---

## INTERMEDIATE BENCHMARKS & RECOMMENDATIONS

**Intermediate Achievement Standards:**
- [ ] First muscle-up (kipping acceptable initially)
- [ ] First full pistol squat each leg
- [ ] 5-second tucked front lever
- [ ] 5 dragon flags with control
- [ ] 15+ strict pull-ups
- [ ] 40+ push-ups
- [ ] 20+ parallel bar dips
- [ ] 20 second L-sit

**Training Split for Intermediate:**

*Option 1: Push/Pull/Legs*
- **Push Day**: Dips, push-up variations, handstand work, planche leans
- **Pull Day**: Pull-ups, muscle-up practice, front lever work, rows
- **Leg Day**: Pistol progressions, squat variations, Nordic curls

*Option 2: Upper/Lower/Skill*
- **Upper**: Push-ups, pull-ups, dips, rows
- **Lower**: Pistol work, squats, lunges, leg curls
- **Skill**: Muscle-up practice, front lever holds, handstand practice

**Progressive Overload in Calisthenics:**
Since you can't add weight easily (though you can via weight vests), use:
1. **More reps**
2. **More sets**
3. **Harder progression**
4. **Slower tempo** (especially eccentrics)
5. **Reduced rest periods**
6. **Pause reps** (hold at hardest point)

**Sources:**
- Overcoming Gravity 2nd Edition (Steven Low)
- Calisthenics Fundamentals (Al Kavadlo & Danny Kavadlo)
- FitnessFAQs (Daniel Vadnal) - YouTube progressions
- GMB Fitness - Skill development
- Tom Merrick (Bodyweight Warrior) - Flexibility for skills
```
